    "5.3 ILLUSTRATION: CYBERATTACKS\n\nalso includes private contractors who are empowered by a state to ‘exercise elements of governmental authority’.⁹² With regard to the attribution of cyberattacks by non-state actors, it is not yet certain what level of control a state should exercise over a non-state actor in order for its cyberattacks to be attributable to the state. The ‘overall control’ test and ‘effective control’ test are tailored to suit responsibility for the acts of organised groups, making them insufficient in the cyber context where attacks can be committed by more unorganised groups, and even by individuals. The Tallinn Manual, therefore, suggests that a state needs to have issued specific instructions or directed or controlled a particular operation to engage state responsibility.⁹³ That such influence has been exercised can, of course, be difficult to prove. The attribution of cyberattacks is particularly difficult as it can be challenging not only to find the particular actor, but also to identify with certainty from which state it is operating.⁹⁴\n\nThe issue of attribution of cyberattacks is of particular significance considering the potential for such attacks to be considered ‘armed attacks’. The importance of setting high evidence thresholds for ‘armed attacks’ cannot be overstated, as a classification of an act as an armed attack creates a right for the target state to use proportionate force in self-defence.⁹⁵ First of all, it should be noted again that the burden of proof lies with the state exercising its right to self-defence. While there is nothing suggesting that attribution is dependent upon a state’s acknowledgement of its involvement, a lack of acknowledgement can lead to significant difficulties when it comes to providing evidence. If there is no sufficient evidence that the state in question was involved in the attack, a use of force against that state would be an unlawful use of force in itself, and a loosening of the evidence requirement would increase the risk of attacks in self-defence being committed against the wrong state (or non-state actor).⁹⁶\n\n⁹² Ibid.\n⁹³ Ibid., 94, rule 17.\n⁹⁴ Schmitt, Tallinn Manual 2.0, n 1, 83. First of all, it is essential to agree upon under what circumstances cyberattacks constitute ‘armed attacks’. Furthermore, cyberattacks also spark particular questions relating to proportionality and to the intent behind attacks. Some argue that all intentional breaches of Article 2(4) of the UN Charter by states will constitute an armed attack giving rise to a right to self-defence.¹⁰² However, the ICJ has suggested that the concept of an armed attack requires the use of force to be of a certain level of gravity, and\n\n⁹⁷ *Corfu Channel* (United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland v Albania) (Judgment) [1949] ICJ Rep 4.\n\n⁹⁸ Ibid.\n\n⁹⁹ Chapter 3, Section 3.4.3.\n\n¹⁰⁰ Including the attack on al-Kibar, as well as other targeted killings of scientists, etc.\n\n¹⁰¹ Christine Gray, ‘Use of Force to Prevent the Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons’ (2009) 2009 Japanese Yearbook of International Law 101; Andrew Garwood-Gowers, ‘Israel’s Airstrike on Syria’s Al-Kibar Facility: A Test Case for the Doctrine of Pre-emptive Self-Defence?’ (2011) 16 Journal of Conflict and Security Law 263.\n\n¹⁰² See especially Elisabeth Wilmshurst, ‘Principles of International Law on the Use of Force by States in Self-Defence’ (working paper ILP WP 05/01, Chatham House, Royal Institute for International Affairs, 2005) 6.\n\nhttps://doi.org/10.1017/9781108637671.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press",
    "5.3 ILLUSTRATION: CYBERATTACKS\n\nhas taken a starting point in the UN General Assembly's Definition of Aggression $^{103}$ when assessing whether an armed attack has occurred, both in the Nicaragua case in 1986, and again in the Armed Activities case in 2005. $^{104}$ The difference in the Court's interpretation of force and armed attack should, however, not be overstated. In the 2003 Oil Platforms case, the ICJ did not rule out the possibility of the destruction of a vessel constituting an armed attack, thus allowing for a quite inclusive interpretation of the term. $^{105}$ In a similar vein, some writers argue that even though there is a difference between armed attack and less grave uses of force, the threshold for an armed attack is lower than that for an act of aggression. $^{106}$ Further, there is an increasing acceptance of 'cumulative' armed attacks, that is, the possibility that a number of small-scale attacks amount to an armed attack giving a right to self-defence. $^{107}$ This is of particular importance regarding cyberattacks as they often are conducted over some time, as seen with both the attacks against Estonia and Iran.\n\n## 5.3.4.2 Cyberattacks and Temporality\n\nAs discussed in the previous chapter, the question of temporality and the potential right to use force in self-defence against imminent threats is one of the most contentious in contemporary debates on jus ad bellum. $^{108}$\n\nIn relation to unacknowledged acts, there has been some discussion about whether Israel has made any claims for a right to use preventive force against the proliferation of nuclear weapons through its attacks on Iran, including the Stuxnet virus and the seemingly related targeted killings. $^{109}$ That these attacks would constitute a claim for non-temporal\n\n$^{103}$ Definition of Aggression, GA Res 3314 (XXIX), UN Doc A/RES/3314 (14 December 1974) annex.\n$^{104}$ Nicaragua (Judgment) [1986] ICJ Rep 14; Armed Activities (Judgment) [2005] ICJ Rep 168.\n$^{105}$ Oil Platforms (Islamic Republic of Iran v United States of America) (Judgment) [2003] ICJ Rep 161, 195 [71] ('Oil Platforms').\n$^{106}$ Tom Ruys, 'Armed Attack' and Article 51 of the UN Charter – Evolutions in Customary Law and Practice (Cambridge University Press, 2010) 139.     "5.3 ILLUSTRATION: CYBERATTACKS\n\nagainst Iran would qualify as a claim for such an interpretation considering the problems discussed.\n\n## 5.3.4.3 Cyberattacks and Necessity\n\nCyberattacks also raise questions for the interpretation of imminence and the closely intertwined customary requirement of necessity. Despite wide agreement that a use of force in self-defence must be necessary,¹¹³ the assessment of the necessity criteria is far from clear and its interpretation can be informed by debates around covert operations, in particular as they apply to cyberattacks.\n\nThe necessity requirement stems from the Caroline formula and requires the threat to ‘leav[e] no choice of means, and no moment for deliberation’,¹¹⁴ and is thus closely intertwined with the concept of imminence of an armed attack discussed earlier. As previously discussed,¹¹⁵ strict application of the requirement of necessity would rule out the greatest dangers of the growing acceptance of pre-emptive self-defence, as force would only be used as a ‘last resort’; that is, only where there is no possibility to resolve the situation with peaceful means, and it is therefore important to pay close attention to this when debating the legality of operations involving force.\n\nIt should be noted that necessity is not just intertwined with the imminence of the armed attack, but also with the imminence of the response; there is a fine line between an act of self-defence and one of (pure) retaliation. A victim state cannot wait a significant amount of time after an armed attack has occurred to strike back when the threat is not ongoing, as such an attack would be of punitive, rather than defensive nature. In relation to cyberattacks, this is particularly complicated considering that it can be difficult to determine the very beginning and end of a cyberattack, or at the very least the part of the attacks amounting to an ‘armed attack’. This, again, ties back to the discussion about approaches to determining cyberattacks as force and developments in this regard will most likely take the form of debates amongst epistemic communities around unacknowledged, but publicly known, attacks.\n\n¹¹³ See, e.g., Nicaragua (Judgment) [1986] ICJ Rep 14, 94 [176]; Legality of the Threat or Use of Nuclear Weapons (Advisory Opinion) [1996] ICJ Rep 226, 245 [41]; Oil Platforms [2003] ICJ Rep 161, 183 [43], 196 [73]–[74], 198 [76]. See also Wilmshurst n 102, 5,7, 10.\nCorrespondence between Great Britain and the United States, respecting the Arrest and Imprisonment of Mr McLeod, for the Destruction of the Steamboat Caroline (March, April 1841) 29 British Foreign and State Papers (1840–41) 1126.\n¹¹⁵ See Chapter 4, Section 4.5.4.2.\n\nhttps://doi.org/10.1017/9781108637671.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press",
    "UNACKNOWLEDGED OPERATIONS\n\n## 5.3.4.4 Cyberattacks and Proportionality\n\nDespite wide agreement that a use of force in self-defence must be proportionate,¹¹⁶ the assessment of this criteria is far from clear, and its interpretation can be informed by debates around covert operations. The issue does not just concern the assessment of a threshold, but also a decision about what to measure the proportionality against. According to the vast majority of scholars, the demand for proportionality requires that the use of force does not go beyond what is needed for the threat to be eliminated.¹¹⁷ Such a functional approach has however been challenged by customary practice which also seems to add an element of ‘matching’ the initial attack.¹¹⁸ This matching does not, however, have to be in terms of similar weapons. This is of particular importance when considering proportionality in response to cyberattacks. As there is a lack of state practice to rely upon, the interpretation of this customary principle in relation to cyberattacks has thus far been left to experts. The Tallinn Manual states that in the event that a state would conduct a cyberattack amounting to an armed attack, the victim state would have the right to use both kinetic force and cyberattacks in response.¹¹⁹\n\nThe difficulty of assessing the proportionality requirement in relation to cyber attacks was illustrated by the Stuxnet virus, where, even after the attack had become publicly known, there was still uncertainty as to the actual damage done to Iran’s nuclear programme. Despite this, it is an important part of *jus ad bellum*, the importance of which can be said to be even greater considering the uncertainty around temporality discussed in the previous chapter. One of the dangers of allowing for the preventive use of force in self-defence against potential future attacks without being certain of where the attack is coming from, or what shape it will take, is that this also expands the possibilities as to what can be considered proportionate to eliminate a threat, which leads to the assessment of the proportionality of the response becoming even more speculative. Indeed, adherence to the proportionality requirement can, together with necessity, act as an aid to restrict the potential dangers of preventive, and pre-emptive self-defence. To leave the interpretation of the proportionality\n\n¹¹⁶ See, e.g., *Nicaragua (Judgment)* [1986] ICJ Rep 14, 94 [176]; *Oil Platforms* [2003] ICJ Rep 161, 183 [43], 196–7 [73]–[74], 198 [76].     "5.4 CYBERATTACKS &amp; DYNAMIC INTERPRETATION\n\nrequirement in relation to cyberattacks entirely to experts is, therefore, problematic and presents a potential danger with state silence in relation to cyberattacks.\n\n## 5.4 Cyberattacks and the Dynamic Interpretation of Jus ad Bellum\n\nIt is clear that the cyberattacks against Estonia and Iran have had great influence on debates around the application of the UN Charter to cyberattacks. Again, to influence the debate is very different from constituting precedents for the development of custom; yet the ideas and arguments put forward in scholarly articles and documents such as the Tallinn Manual around unacknowledged cyberattacks can shape the thinking of governmental lawyers and possibly also states, thus indirectly shaping the development of customary international law. Further, as previously discussed, international legal scholars, and other actors are part of the 'process of interpretation' of the rules on the use of force.¹²⁰ This is not a novel phenomenon; indeed the Tallinn Manual is 'part of a longstanding tradition of legal scholars and practitioners laboring to adapt existing law to new circumstances, opting to extend the law by way of interpretation and analogy'.¹²¹ The aim here is therefore not to evaluate the Manual's value as a 'normative point of reference',¹²² but rather it is to illustrate the role of covert operations in illuminating and creating the 'gap' which the Manual, and other scholarly interventions, seeks to fill.¹²³ It is important to recognise how the role for legal scholars becomes increasingly important and extensive as more and more of the legal discussion involves acts that are covert in whole or in part, where\n\n¹²⁰ See, e.g., Ingo Venzke, How Interpretation Makes International Law: On Semantic Change and Normative Twists (Oxford University Press, 2012) 18.\n¹²¹ Efrony and Shany, n 37, 583\n¹²² Ibid.\n¹²³ Interesting and insightful recent writings on scholarship and knowledge production in international law include Jörg Kammerhofer, 'Lawmaking by Scholars' in Catherine Brölmann and Yannick Radi (eds.), Research Handbook on the Theory and Practice of International Lawmaking (Edward Elgar, 2016) 305; Lianne JM Boer, International Law As We Know It: Cyberwar Discourse and the Construction of Knowledge in International Legal Scholarship (PhD dissertation, Vrije Universiteit Amsterdam, 2017); Matthew Windsor, 'Narrative Kill or Capture: Unreliable Narration in International Law' (2015) 28 Leiden Journal of International Law 743; Jean d'Aspremont, Epistemic Forces in International Law: Foundational Doctrines and Techniques of International Legal Argumentation (Edward Elgar Publishing, 2015); Carsten Stahn and Eric De Brabandere, \"The Future of International Legal Scholarship: Some Thoughts on \"Practice\", \"Growth\", and \"Dissemination\" (2014) 27 Leiden Journal of International Law 1.\n\nhttps://doi.org/10.1017/9781108637671.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press",
    "UNACKNOWLEDGED OPERATIONS\n\nreactions to and analyses of reports of acts of force take place before there have been any justifications or reactions presented by states, rather than limiting the role of international legal scholars to evaluating the validity of legal arguments presented by states.\n\nAs discussed by Jean d'Aspremont, the space for ‘interventionism’ by legal scholars is particularly evident in relation to cyberattacks.¹²⁴ As there is disagreement both as to what threshold to use, and through which approach to address this threshold, any incidents that lead to debates providing clarifications on this will likely have significant impact on how cyberattacks will continue to be incorporated into international law on the use of force. Where there is uncertainty as to how to interpret ‘force’ or ‘armed attack’ and there is no relevant practice by states or UN organs on which to turn, it is likely to be the case that scholarly projects discussing unacknowledged attacks will prove to have great influence over the way in which the rules will be interpreted in the future.¹²⁵ As mentioned concerning the Tallinn Manual, however, it is also important to remember that such developments risk centring power on a small and select number of international legal experts.\n\n## 5.5 Conclusion\n\nAs demonstrated, while unacknowledged operations are often not of direct relevance in themselves, debates around them can be of great relevance for the development of international law on the use of force. Any assessment of the value of reactions to unacknowledged acts is connected to the value attributed to verbal and physical state practice and the requirement for debate to be grounded in public physical practice. For states and international lawyers who accept verbal statements as relevant for legal development (as most do), covert operations may serve\n\n¹²⁴ Jean d'Aspremont, ‘Cyber Operations and International Law: An Interventionist Legal Thought’ (2016) 21 Journal of Conflict and Security Law 575. See also Pål Wrange, ‘Intervention in National and Private Cyberspace and International Law’ in Jonas Ebbesson et al. (eds.), International Law and Changing Perceptions of Security (Brill Nijhoff, 2014) 307, stressing the urgency of clarifying what ‘terms like “use of force” … mean in cyberspace’ and further arguing that ‘[i]n that process, commentators on international law should play an important role’: at 326.\n\n¹²⁵ See, e.g., Alexandra Perina on the value of the Tallinn Manual, stating that: ‘In the absence of comparably detailed State articulations of the law, it is likely to be the resource of first resort in its field to practitioners and scholars alike’: Alexandra H Perina, ‘Black Holes and Open Secrets: The Impact of Covert Action on International Law’ (2015) 53 Columbia Journal of Transnational Law 507, 582.\n\nhttps://doi.org/10.1017/9781108637671.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press",
    "5.5 CONCLUSION\n\nas a trigger for such practice, as well as for expressions of how reacting states understand the operations in relation to international law. At the same time, state silence in response to reports of unacknowledged conduct should not be interpreted as acquiescence, as states generally will not be of the understanding that such conduct is in a position to alter the law.\n\nDespite problems with finding ‘a’ scholarly interpretation of a certain concept or rule, or deciding which of the offered interpretations to adopt, scholarship still assists in the interpretation of the Charter rules and affects the way in which states will address related issues in the future. This is especially so for areas where states remain completely silent, and there is a need to update the interpretation of the law in light of novel developments where state practice is lacking.\n\nUnacknowledged operations that are publicly known illustrate the need to move beyond debates of state practice and customary international law when assessing the role that such operations may play in the development of *jus ad bellum*. Whilst, as previously discussed, states and UN organs are the primary interpreters of the UN Charter, numerous other actors participate in this development. Even though the core institutional framework and authorities remain, covert but publicly known conduct opens up more space for some actors, such as legal scholars and other experts to influence the development of the law.\n\nhttps://doi.org/10.1017/9781108637671.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press"
